Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Why Truthfulness and Justice Are Not Intrinsically Good
Buti answers an objection using examples of truthfulness and justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti answers the objection that certain actions like truthfulness and justice seem inherently good, by showing their goodness depends on external factors created by God.

Core Idea
Truthfulness is not good in itself; its goodness comes from benefits, reward, and created human nature.

This lesson addresses a natural objection to the Ash'ari view: if good and evil are conventional, how can we explain that truthfulness and justice are universally considered good? Buti responds by showing that the reasons for their goodness are all external to the acts themselves—such as worldly benefits, religious reward, and human disposition—all created by God and therefore contingent. He further argues that God could alter human nature to reverse these valuations, proving their conventionality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the part where Buti asks how the goodness of truthfulness and justice can be conventional. This is the objection he will answer.
The objection is phrased as 'كيف أفهم أن حسن الصدق والعدل اعتباري'.
2
Analyze the three external causes for truthfulness
Buti lists three reasons: benefits, reward, and natural disposition. Notice each is outside the essence of truthfulness.
All three are created by God and could be changed.
3
Apply the same logic to justice
Buti extends the argument to justice, showing that the goodness of giving rights depends on God's creating humans with needs.
If God created humans without needs, those actions would not be just.
4
Reflect on the radical implication
Buti concludes that God could reverse human dispositions entirely, proving that moral valuations are not fixed but dependent on His creation.
This reinforces the conventionality of good and evil.

Commentary
This passage is a kalam proof that good and evil are not intrinsic (dhatī) but conventional (i'tibārī). The author begins by stating the title: 'Nothing is obligatory upon God, and good and evil in things are conventional.' He then explains that this follows logically from the previously established truth that there is no intermediary between God and creation — God is the direct creator of everything, including all attributes and accidents. If this is so, then things cannot inherently contain good or evil, because good and evil are also created by God. Instead, God creates the thing and then creates the meaning of good or evil and connects them. Therefore, the good or evil of an action is not a necessary property of that action but a judgment (hukm) from God, which could be reversed if He willed. The author anticipates an objection: how can we understand that truthfulness and justice are not inherently good? He answers by enumerating the external causes for why we consider them good: truthfulness leads to benefits, brings reward in the afterlife, and aligns with human natural disposition (fitra) to respect truth and abhor lies. All these reasons are external to the act itself. He further argues that God could change human nature to reverse these dispositions, proving that our moral valuations are contingent on God's creation. He extends the example to justice, showing that what we consider rights depend on God's having created humans with needs. If God had created humans differently, those same actions would not be considered good or just. Thus, the conclusion stands: good and evil are conventional, rooted in God's will and creation, not in the essence of things.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari view denies that truthfulness is objectively good or that lying is objectively evil.
Correction: Buti affirms that truthfulness is good and lying is evil, but he shows that this goodness is not inherent in the acts themselves; it is created by God and could be otherwise.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, which of the following is NOT a reason for the goodness of truthfulness?
A) Truthfulness leads to worldly benefits.
B) Truthfulness is rewarded in the afterlife.
C) Truthfulness is logically necessary for society.
D) Human souls are naturally disposed to respect truthfulness.

Key Takeaway
The goodness of truthfulness and justice depends on external factors created by God, not on any intrinsic property of the acts themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If truthfulness is only good because God created human nature to love it, do you ever rely on that nature to be truthful when it costs you something, or do you only tell the truth when it benefits you? Are you willing to be truthful even when your fitra might be corrupted by habit, knowing that God could change your nature? How can you cultivate a love for truthfulness as a discipline, not just a natural inclination?
?
Justice is defined by rights that God created us to need. Think of someone you have wronged by denying them a right—perhaps a right you didn't feel they deserved. If God could have made that right non-existent, does that lessen your responsibility? Or does it make you more accountable, because God did create that need? What injustice are you tolerating in your life that you have convinced yourself is 'not really' unjust?
?
Buti says God could have changed human nature so that we love lying. That means your current dislike for lying is a gift from God. When was the last time you thanked God for creating you with a sound fitra that inclines you to truth? And if you ever feel your fitra turning hard or indifferent, what does this lesson teach you about the possibility of transformation?

Divine Attributes

God Is Not Compelled by Any External Standard
Buti refutes the misconception of inherent moral values and establishes God's freedom from compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be compelled by any standards of good and evil, since He is the one who defines them.

Core Idea
God defines good and evil; He cannot be bound by them.

We often mistakenly think that goodness and evil are inherent in actions themselves. But Buti argues that once we recognize that Allah alone assigns moral value, we see that He is not compelled to follow any external standard. This lesson explores that crucial insight.

Guided Path
1
Understand the mistaken perception
The passage begins by noting our familiarity with causal links makes us think good/evil are inherent. Notice how Buti sets up the problem.
2
Grasp the true source of moral qualities
Buti states that Allah makes things good or evil by His will. This is key to understanding His freedom.
3
Conclusion: God is not compelled
If He defines good/evil, then He cannot be compelled by them. He is not bound to choose the best; He is beyond such compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن من شدة إلفنا للترابط الذي خلقه الله بين الأشياء وخواصها ، نظن أن معنى الحسن أو القبح قد غدا كامنا في ذات كل منهما فهما لا ينفكان بعضهما عن بعض . فإذا أدركت هذه الحقيقة إدراكاً جيداً ، علمت أن الله تعالى ليس مجبوراً في خلقه وفي حكمه على أي شيء ، إذ لو كان مجبوراً عليه ، لكان سبب الجبر هو ضرورة اتباعه الأصلح والأفضل ، وتجنبه عن الفاسد والقبيح ؛ وقد علمت أن الذي جعل الصالح صالحاً والفاسد فاسداً والقبيح قبيحاً هو الله عز وجل وأنه لاشيء يسمى بالنظر لذاته حسناً أو قبيحاً وأن الأمور كلها بالنسبة إلى الله في بدء الخلق سواء . فجائز على الله تعالى أن لا يثيب الطائع ويعذبه ، وأن لا يعذب الكافر به ويثيبه . ولا يقال إن ذلك مناف للحكمة والمصلحة ، لأن الذي جعل الشيء حكمة أو مصلحة هو الله عز وجل ، فلا يعقل أن يتصف شيء من أعماله بأنه مناف للمصلحة . غير أننا نقول : إن الله كتب على نفسه في صريح كتابه ، أن يثيب الطائع ، لطفاً منه ورحمة . فلا بد أن ينفذ وعده لأنه أخبرنا بذلك ولأنه أصدق الصادقين ، ولأنه جعل الصدق بشرعه حسناً والكذب قبيحاً .
And due to our intense familiarity with the interconnection that Allah created between things and their properties, we think that the meaning of goodness or evil has become inherent in the essence of each, so that they are inseparable from one another. But when you grasp this fact well, you will know that Allah Almighty is not compelled in His creation or in His ruling concerning anything, for if He were compelled, the cause of compulsion would be the necessity of His following what is most beneficial and best, and avoiding what is corrupt and evil. And you already know that the One who made the good good, the corrupt corrupt, and the evil evil is Allah Almighty, and that there is nothing that is considered by its essence good or evil, and that all matters are equal for Allah at the beginning of creation. Thus, it is possible for Allah Almighty not to reward the obedient and to punish him, and not to punish the disbeliever and to reward him. And it cannot be said that this contradicts wisdom and benefit, because the One who makes a thing wisdom or benefit is Allah Almighty, so it is inconceivable that any of His actions be described as contradicting benefit. However, we say: Allah has prescribed upon Himself in His clear Book that He will reward the obedient, out of His grace and mercy. So He must fulfill His promise because He informed us of that, and because He is the most truthful of the truthful, and because He made truthfulness good by His law and lying evil.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion drawn from the previous argument about good and evil being considerational. The author notes that due to our habitual familiarity with the causal connections Allah has established, we mistakenly think that goodness and evil are inherent in actions. But if one truly grasps that Allah alone determines moral qualities, one realises that Allah is not compelled by any external standard. If He were compelled, the compulsion would mean He must always choose the best and avoid evil—but since He Himself defines what is good and evil, He cannot be bound by them. Therefore, in terms of pure possibility, Allah could even reward a disbeliever and punish a believer without contradiction to His wisdom, because He defines wisdom itself. However, the author clarifies that Allah has voluntarily promised in the Qur'an to reward the obedient and punish the disobedient out of His grace and mercy. This promise is binding because Allah is truthful and He made truthfulness good by His law. So the moral order is established by divine decree and promise, not by any necessity independent of God.

Key Terms
مجبور
الأصلح
الأفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must follow a pre-existing standard of good and evil.
Correction: God defines good and evil, so He is not compelled by any external standard.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, what leads us to mistakenly think good/evil are inherent?
A) Our familiarity with causal connections
B) Divine revelation
C) Philosophical reasoning
D) Religious texts

Key Takeaway
God is not compelled by any external moral standard because He alone defines what is good, evil, wise, or beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
In moments of trial, do you believe that God has not chosen the best for you? How can you train your heart to trust that whatever He wills is wise by His definition?

God's Voluntary Promise and the Moral Order
Buti explains God's voluntary promise to reward the obedient and why it is binding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's voluntary promise in the Qur'an establishes a binding moral order without compulsion.

Core Idea
God voluntarily binds Himself to reward and punish through His truthful promise.

While God is not compelled by any external standard, He has mercifully promised in the Qur'an to reward the obedient. This promise is binding because God is truthful and He made truthfulness good. In this lesson, we explore how this establishes a reliable moral order.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the shift in argument
After establishing God's freedom, Buti now addresses the question of whether this implies arbitrariness. Notice the phrase 'However, we say' signals a new point.
2
Understand the basis of divine promise
God prescribed upon Himself to reward obedience. This is an act of grace, not compulsion.
3
Why is the promise binding?
Because God is truthful and He made truthfulness good by His law. This ensures His promises are reliable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن من شدة إلفنا للترابط الذي خلقه الله بين الأشياء وخواصها ، نظن أن معنى الحسن أو القبح قد غدا كامنا في ذات كل منهما فهما لا ينفكان بعضهما عن بعض . فإذا أدركت هذه الحقيقة إدراكاً جيداً ، علمت أن الله تعالى ليس مجبوراً في خلقه وفي حكمه على أي شيء ، إذ لو كان مجبوراً عليه ، لكان سبب الجبر هو ضرورة اتباعه الأصلح والأفضل ، وتجنبه عن الفاسد والقبيح ؛ وقد علمت أن الذي جعل الصالح صالحاً والفاسد فاسداً والقبيح قبيحاً هو الله عز وجل وأنه لاشيء يسمى بالنظر لذاته حسناً أو قبيحاً وأن الأمور كلها بالنسبة إلى الله في بدء الخلق سواء . فجائز على الله تعالى أن لا يثيب الطائع ويعذبه ، وأن لا يعذب الكافر به ويثيبه . ولا يقال إن ذلك مناف للحكمة والمصلحة ، لأن الذي جعل الشيء حكمة أو مصلحة هو الله عز وجل ، فلا يعقل أن يتصف شيء من أعماله بأنه مناف للمصلحة . غير أننا نقول : إن الله كتب على نفسه في صريح كتابه ، أن يثيب الطائع ، لطفاً منه ورحمة . فلا بد أن ينفذ وعده لأنه أخبرنا بذلك ولأنه أصدق الصادقين ، ولأنه جعل الصدق بشرعه حسناً والكذب قبيحاً .
And due to our intense familiarity with the interconnection that Allah created between things and their properties, we think that the meaning of goodness or evil has become inherent in the essence of each, so that they are inseparable from one another. But when you grasp this fact well, you will know that Allah Almighty is not compelled in His creation or in His ruling concerning anything, for if He were compelled, the cause of compulsion would be the necessity of His following what is most beneficial and best, and avoiding what is corrupt and evil. And you already know that the One who made the good good, the corrupt corrupt, and the evil evil is Allah Almighty, and that there is nothing that is considered by its essence good or evil, and that all matters are equal for Allah at the beginning of creation. Thus, it is possible for Allah Almighty not to reward the obedient and to punish him, and not to punish the disbeliever and to reward him. And it cannot be said that this contradicts wisdom and benefit, because the One who makes a thing wisdom or benefit is Allah Almighty, so it is inconceivable that any of His actions be described as contradicting benefit. However, we say: Allah has prescribed upon Himself in His clear Book that He will reward the obedient, out of His grace and mercy. So He must fulfill His promise because He informed us of that, and because He is the most truthful of the truthful, and because He made truthfulness good by His law and lying evil.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion drawn from the previous argument about good and evil being considerational. The author notes that due to our habitual familiarity with the causal connections Allah has established, we mistakenly think that goodness and evil are inherent in actions. But if one truly grasps that Allah alone determines moral qualities, one realises that Allah is not compelled by any external standard. If He were compelled, the compulsion would mean He must always choose the best and avoid evil—but since He Himself defines what is good and evil, He cannot be bound by them. Therefore, in terms of pure possibility, Allah could even reward a disbeliever and punish a believer without contradiction to His wisdom, because He defines wisdom itself. However, the author clarifies that Allah has voluntarily promised in the Qur'an to reward the obedient and punish the disobedient out of His grace and mercy. This promise is binding because Allah is truthful and He made truthfulness good by His law. So the moral order is established by divine decree and promise, not by any necessity independent of God.

رحمة
صدق
حسن
قبيح

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's promise to reward is arbitrary and could change at any time.
Correction: God's promise is binding because He is truthful and He made truthfulness good, making it reliable.

Checkpoint
Why does Buti say God must fulfill His promise?
A) Because He is compelled by justice
B) Because He is truthful and made truthfulness good
C) Because He fears contradiction
D) Because the angels enforce it

Key Takeaway
God's promise to reward the obedient is binding because He is truthful and He made truthfulness good, not because He is compelled.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God's promise to reward the obedient is absolutely certain? What would change in your life if you truly believed that Allah cannot break His promise? Consider a specific act of obedience you have been neglecting. What does your hesitation reveal about your trust in Allah's truthfulness? Write one concrete step you will take today to act on this certainty.
?
The source says Allah made truthfulness good by His law. In what areas of your life have you been making exceptions to truthfulness? How does your comfort with small deceptions reflect on your understanding of Allah's own truthfulness? Commit to one situation where you will choose truth even if it costs you.
?
If Allah's promise is based on His grace and mercy, not compulsion, how does that affect your relationship with Him? Do you approach obedience as a transaction or as a response to grace? Examine your heart: are you more motivated by fear of punishment or hope in His promise? Ask yourself which motive is stronger in your daily choices.

God Creates the Ugly and Harmful
Buti affirms God's creation of the ugly/harmful as part of His creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is valid to say that God creates the ugly and harmful, and how this fits within the Ash'ari view of divine creation.

Core Idea
God's creation includes all properties and effects, even those we perceive as ugly or harmful.

Buti continues his series of points by addressing a sensitive issue: the attribution of evil or harm to God's creation. In this passage, he explains the reasoning behind saying 'God created the ugly or harmful,' grounding it in the Ash'ari principle that God is the creator of all things, including their properties and outcomes. Let's read his concise but precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the concise style and logical flow.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the main claim
Recognize that the author's key claim is the truth of the statement 'God created the ugly or harmful.' This is the second point in his series.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Break down the three examples the author gives: instilling specific properties, driving to harmful results, and creating repulsion in temperaments. Each shows how God's creative act includes outcomes we consider negative.
These examples are not exhaustive but illustrative.
4
Understand the conclusion
Grasp that the author concludes that these instances mean God created the 'ugly' within His overall creation, affirming that nothing exists outside His creative will.
This aligns with Ash'ari theology that all existence is from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : أنه يصدق قولنا إذا بأن الله خلق القبيح أو الضار . لأنه عندما ركب في الأشياء خصائص معينة أو ساقها إلى نتائج ذات تأثير معين تخالف مصالح الناس ، أو خلق في الأمزجة اشمئزازاً منها ؛ فمعنى ذلك أنه قد خلق القبيح ، ضمن ما خلقه من المكونات
The second point: It is true that we say that God created the ugly or harmful. Because when He instilled in things specific properties or drove them to results with a specific effect that conflict with people's interests, or created in temperaments a repulsion from them; then the meaning of that is that He created the ugly, within what He created of things.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is the second point in a series of arguments. The author states that it is valid to say that God created the ugly or harmful. The reason is that God is the creator of all properties and effects in the world. When He instills in objects certain characteristics that lead to harmful outcomes for people, or when He creates in human temperaments a natural aversion to certain things, then He is essentially creating the ugly or harmful as part of His creation. The author uses the phrase 'ضمن ما خلقه من المكونات' (within what He created of things) to emphasize that these ugly or harmful aspects are part of the overall created order, not something separate. This affirms the Ash'ari position that God is the creator of all things, both good and evil in terms of their existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that saying 'God created evil' implies that God is evil.
Correction: The author only affirms that God creates the existence of harmful things, not that He is described by evil.

Checkpoint

A) Because God directly wills evil.
B) Because He instilled harmful properties in things.
C) Because He is the only creator and evil is an illusion.
D) Because He is not responsible for evil.

Key Takeaway
God creates all properties and effects, including those we perceive as ugly or harmful, as part of His comprehensive creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The passage says God drove things to results that conflict with people's interests. Think of a time when your interests conflicted with God's decree. How does knowing He created that conflict affect your trust in Him? Will you submit to His creation even when it hurts, and seek His pleasure in that submission?

Creating Evil Is Not a Divine Deficiency
Buti argues that creating evil is not a deficiency, refutes the objection of injustice, and concludes that reason alone cannot judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creating evil and harm in the universe is not a deficiency in God, and how to refute the objection that God's actions could be unjust.

Core Idea
Creating evil is a perfect act; being evil would be a deficiency.

Buti addresses a common objection: if God is all-good, why does He create evil and suffering? He argues that creating evil is actually a manifestation of God's perfect will—He creates whatever He wills without restriction. The deficiency would only exist if God Himself were described with evil. He then tackles the issue of injustice, distinguishing God's absolute ownership from human transactions, and concludes that reason alone cannot judge God's actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Buti's argument flow naturally before you interpret.
Notice the clear logical progression from assertion to objection to conclusion.
2
Distinguish creating from being
Identify the key distinction Buti makes: creating evil is not a deficiency, but being described by evil would be. Find the examples he gives (weakness, lying).
3
Examine the objection and answer
Buti anticipates an objection about injustice. How does he respond? Note the difference between human ownership and God's absolute dominion.
The doubt arises from illicit analogy.
4
Understand the conclusion
Buti concludes that reason alone cannot determine divine judgments. This supports the doctrine that there is no moral obligation before revelation.
This is a key Ash'ari principle.
5
Reflect on the Qur'anic verses
Buti quotes two verses about trial and testing. How do these verses support his point about hidden wisdom?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : ليس من صفات النقص التي علمنا أن الله منزه عنها ، أن يكون قد خلق القبيح والضار في الكون . لأن من صفات الكمال الثابتة لله أنه يخلق ما يشاء دون أن يصده عن ذلك أي شيء ، قوة كان أو عرفاً أو قانوناً . وليس خلقه لأصناف الموجودات من قبيح وحسن وضار ونافع إلا مظهراً لهذه الصفة الكاملة . ولكن المنافي لصفة الكمال والمستلزم للنقص ، أن يقال إنه اكتسب القبيح أو اتصف به . وفرق كبير بين هذا وذاك . ليس نقصاً في ذات الله أن يخلق العجز في الكون متمثلاً في شتى المظاهر، ولكن النقص أن يتصف هو بشيء من العجز . وليس قبيحاً أن يخلق الله الكذب ) ظاهرة يتصف بها بعض الناس ) ولكن القبيح أن يتلبس هو بشيء من هذا الكذب الذي خلقه ، لا لأنه قبيح بحد ذاته عقلاً ، فقد أثبتنا بطلان ذلك ، ولكن لأنه متلبس بمعان ومستلزمات لا تتفق ومصالح العباد ولأن الله جعله بشرعه قبيحاً . فلا يمكن أن يتصف البارئ جل جلاله به (1) ولا ينبغي أن يلتبس عليك الأمر فتقول : وقد جعل الظلم أيضاً بشرعه قبيحاً ، فلا ينبغي أن يتصف بالظلم ، وإذا فلا ينبغي أن يعذب الطائع ، أو يبتلي الناس بمصائب دون جريرة اقترفوها - لأن الظلم هو أن تتصرف بشيء يخص غيرك بدون رضاه ، فهذا هو الذي قبحه الشرع أما تصرف الله عز وجل بمخلوقاته ، فليس من ذلك في شيء ، بل هو إنما يتصرف بملكه الذي له المشيئة المطلقة فيه كما لا يخفى عليك . والشبهة إنما تطوف بذهنك ، بسبب قياسك ذات الله تعالى على نفسك وعلى ما تواضع عليه عرف الناس في مجتمعاتهم ، فهذا الذي تواضع عليه عرف الناس إنما هو جزء يسير جداً من تكوين الله وخلقه ، ولا يمكن بحال من الأحوال أن يكون أي جزء من أجزاء هذه المخلوقات حاكماً على إرادة الخالق وتصرفاته على أن هذا الذي تراه في الكون من مظاهر البؤس والابتلاء والمصائب التي يصاب بها كثير من الناس ، والتي يسميها البعض حسب اصطلاحنا في التعامل بعضنا ( ظلماً ) ينطوي على حكم ومصالح قد تغيب عنا ، وليس من شرط مع صحتها أن نكون على علم بها واطلاع عليها ، كما أنه ليس من شرط مشروعيتها أن توقع عقولنا عليها بالموافقة والرضا وحسبك مظهراً من مظاهر الحكمة أن تتأمل قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَنَبْلُوكُمْ بِالشَّرِّ وَالخَيْر فِتْنَةٌ وإلينا ترجعون ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٣٥] وقوله عز وجل (وَجَعَلْنا بَعْضَكُمْ لِبَعْضٍ فِتْنَةٌ ، أَتَصْبِرُونَ ؟ وَكانَ رَبُّكَ بَصيراً ﴾ [الفرقان : ٢٠ ] ويترتب على كل ما ذكرناه أن العقل بمفرده لا يستطيع أن يستظهر حكم الله في الأشياء بموجب ما يتراءى فيها من صفة الحسن أو القبح . لأن ماتراه فيها من هذه الصفة ليس ضرورة عقلية ملازمة للذات ، بحيث لابد أن يكون حكم الله تابعاً لها ، وإنما هو ارتباط جعلي أو تصور خيالي بسبب ارتباط تلك الأشياء بما ذكرناه من المصالح الظاهرة الخارجة عنها ، وقد لا يأتي حكم الله على وفقها ولذلك اتفق جمهور المسلمين على أنه لا شرع قبل بعثة الرسل ولا تكليف ، وأن أهل الفترة الذين انقطعوا عن خبر الأنبياء السابقين وبعثة خاتم الأنبياء محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام ليسوا مؤاخذين ولا مكلفين . ويدل عليه قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَما
The third point: It is not from the attributes of deficiency that we know God is exalted above, that He created the ugly and harmful in the universe. Because it is from the attributes of perfection established for God that He creates whatever He wills without anything preventing Him from that, be it power, custom, or law. And His creation of the types of existents, ugly and good, harmful and beneficial, is only a manifestation of this perfect attribute. But what is opposed to the attribute of perfection and entails deficiency, is that it be said that He acquired the ugly or was described by it. And there is a great difference between this and that. It is not a deficiency in God's essence that He creates weakness in the universe, manifested in various forms, but the deficiency is that He be described with any weakness. And it is not ugly that God creates lying (a phenomenon that some people are described by), but the ugly is that He be clothed with any of this lying that He created, not because it is ugly in itself intellectually – for we have proven the invalidity of that – but because it is clothed with meanings and implications that do not agree with the interests of the servants and because God made it ugly by His law. So it is impossible that the Creator, glorified be His majesty, be described with it. (1) And you should not let the matter be confused such that you say: He also made injustice ugly by His law, so He should not be described with injustice, and if so, He should not punish the obedient, or afflict people with calamities without crimes they committed – because injustice is to dispose of something belonging to another without his consent, that is what the law made ugly. As for the disposal of God Almighty with His creatures, it is not part of that at all; rather, He only disposes of His possession over which He has absolute will, as is not hidden from you. And the doubt only circulates in your mind because of your analogy of God Almighty to yourself and to what the custom of people has agreed upon in their societies. For this which the custom of people has agreed upon is only a very small part of God's formation and creation, and it is in no way possible that any part of these creatures be a ruler over the will and actions of the Creator. Moreover, what you see in the universe of manifestations of misery, trial, and calamities that afflict many people, and which some call 'injustice' according to our convention in dealing, contains wisdom and interests that may be hidden from us, and it is not a condition for their validity that we be aware of them, just as it is not a condition for their legitimacy that our minds agree and be satisfied with them. And sufficient as a manifestation of wisdom is that you ponder His saying, the Most High: 'And We test you with evil and good as a trial, and to Us you will return' [Quran 21:35], and His saying, the Mighty and Majestic: 'And We have made some of you as a trial for others: will you be patient? And your Lord is All-Seeing' [Quran 25:20]. And it follows from all that we have mentioned that the intellect alone cannot deduce God's judgment concerning things according to what appears in them of the attribute of good or ugly. Because what you see in them of this attribute is not an intellectual necessity inherent in the essence, such that God's judgment must necessarily follow it; rather, it is an conventional connection or an imaginary conception due to the connection of those things with what we mentioned of apparent interests external to them, and God's judgment may not come in accordance with them. Therefore, the majority of Muslims agreed that there is no law before the sending of messengers and no obligation, and that the people of the interval (ahl al-fatrah) who were cut off from the news of the previous prophets and the mission of the Seal of the Prophets Muhammad, peace be upon him, are not held accountable nor obligated. And evidence for that is His saying, the Most High: 'And We ...'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is the third point in a series. The author argues that creating evil and harm in the universe is not a deficiency in God. Rather, it is a manifestation of His perfect attribute of creating whatever He wills without restriction. The deficiency would be if God Himself were described by evil or acquired it. The author gives examples: creating weakness in the world is not a deficiency, but being weak is; creating lying in some people is not ugly, but God being described with lying would be ugly, not because lying is inherently evil by reason (the author states this has been proven false), but because being described with it entails meanings incompatible with divine perfection and because God's law declares it ugly. The author then addresses a potential objection: if injustice is declared ugly by divine law, then God should not be described with injustice, so He should not punish the obedient or afflict people without cause. The answer is that God's actions are different from human actions of injustice because God acts within His own dominion with absolute will; injustice defined by human custom does not apply to Him. The doubt arises from illicit analogy. Moreover, apparent calamities contain hidden wisdom and interests. The author quotes Quranic verses about testing and trial. The conclusion is that reason alone cannot determine divine judgments, as the connection between things and their moral qualities is conventional, not necessary. This supports the Ash'ari doctrine that there is no moral obligation before revelation, and thus the people of the interval are not held accountable. The passage ends with a partial Quranic verse.

Key Terms
خلق القبيح
صفات النقص
صفات الكمال
اكتسب القبيح
الظلم
أهل الفترة
لا شرع قبل البعثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since God created evil, He is evil or unjust.
Correction: Buti clarifies that creating evil is a manifestation of God's perfect will and does not imply that God is described with evil. God's actions are above human standards of justice; apparent injustices contain hidden wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it not a deficiency for God to create evil and harm?
A) Because evil is an illusion and not real.
B) Because creating is a perfection, while being described by evil would be a deficiency.
C) Because God only creates good, and evil is from people.
D) Because evil does not exist in the universe.

Key Takeaway
Creating evil is not a deficiency in God; the deficiency would be if God were described with evil, and God's actions are above human standards of justice, so reason alone cannot judge them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a personal tragedy or see a calamity that seems utterly unfair, does your heart whisper that God has been unjust? How will you train yourself to respond with the trust that His wisdom surpasses your understanding, as this lesson teaches?
?
Are there areas of your life where you judge God's actions by human standards—expecting reward for good deeds and protection from hardship? How can you surrender that expectation and accept that His will is not bound by your sense of fairness?
?
The lesson says God creates lying in some people but is not a liar Himself. Do you ever fall into the trap of resenting others for their faults while forgetting that God is the Creator of all actions? How can you redirect your focus from blaming creation to trusting the Creator's perfect wisdom?

Human Free Will and Divine Will: No Conflict
Buti explains how human free will coexists with God's absolute will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how human free will is compatible with God's absolute will, as explained by Buti's distinction between involuntary and voluntary actions and the concept of the 'wondrous secret'.

Core Idea
God's will encompasses human free will without conflict, because He wills us to be choosers.

This passage tackles a fundamental question in Islamic theology: if God's will is absolute, how can humans have real choice? Buti guides us through a solution by distinguishing two types of human actions and showing that God's will actually includes the gift of choice itself. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the initial question and the structure of Buti's answer.
Pay attention to the two types of actions he mentions.
2
Identify the two types of actions
Buti divides human actions into two categories. Find them and note examples for each.
3
Understand the 'wondrous secret'
The second type arises from a 'wondrous secret' which is the faculty of choice. See how Buti describes it as the axis of moral responsibility.
4
Follow the resolution
Buti argues that God's will has attached to your being a willing agent. This means God wills your choices, so no conflict arises.
Try to see the logical step: if God wills you to will, then any action you will is also willed by God.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The supposition that God might not will a chosen action is contradictory because it would imply God does not will your will, which He has willed.

Source Text

٤ - مصير الإرادة الإنسانية أمام إرادة الله جل جلاله
والآن ، وقد علمنا بأن إرادة الله تعالى مطلقة وكاملة ، وصالحة للتعلق بكل الممكنات ، فكيف نتصور أن تكون للإنسان أيضاً إرادة إلى جانبها ؟ وقد علمنا ببراهين التجربة والمشاهدة أن الإنسان يريد ويختار في كثير من سلوكه وتصوراته ، فما نوع هذه الإرادة وحقيقتها بل ما مصيرها في جنب إرادة الله ؟ والجواب أن الله عز وجل لما خلق الإنسان ، أقامه على نوعين من الحركة والتصرف ، أما أحدهما فيستوي فيه الإنسان مع سائر الموجودات الأخرى من حيوانات و جمادات ونبات وأفلاك : حركات قسرية ووظائف آلية ليس للإنسان فيها أي كسب أو مشيئة ، كحركة النمو وما يتبعه من قوة وشيب وضعف ، وكالولادة والموت ، وكالانفعالات المختلفة من حب وكراهية وجوع وعطش وخوف وفزع
وأما النوع الثاني منها ، فتصرفات تنشأ من سر عجيب خاص أودعه الله عز وجل في الإنسان ، نسميه : الاختيار والإرادة . فلقد تعلقت إرادة الله عز وجل بأن يغرس في كيان الإنسان هذا السر الذي هو محور التكليف فيه وأن يجعله يصدر في كثير من تصرفاته عن هذا السر الذي به يسمى حراً ومختاراً .
ومعنى ذلك أن إرادة الله تعالى تعلقت بأن تكون مريداً ، فسرت إرادة الله عز وجل - بذلك ـ إلى كل ماتريده وتختاره من الأعمال . وإذا فلا يمكن أن يقع أي تعارض بين إرادة الله تعالى وما تختاره عن طريق إرادتك الخاصة ، إذ لو فرضنا أن الله غير مريد لعمل قد اخترته بإرادتك ، فمعنى ذلك أنه سبحانه وتعالى غير مريد لإرادتك التي وجهتك إلى ذلك الفعل ، وهو مناقض لما ثبت من أن الله عز وجل قد شاء لك أن تكون مريداً وشاء أن يخلق فيك هذا السر ، فثبت بطلان فرض أن الله قد لا يريد العمل الذي تختاره .
4 - The Fate of Human Will Before the Will of God, glorified be His majesty. And now that we know that the will of God Almighty is absolute and perfect, and capable of relating to all possible things, how can we conceive that man also has a will alongside it? We have known through proofs of experience and observation that man wills and chooses in much of his conduct and conceptions. So what is the type of this will and its reality, rather what is its fate alongside the will of God? The answer is that God Almighty, when He created man, established him upon two types of movement and action. As for one of them, man is equal in it to other existents such as animals, inanimate objects, plants, and celestial spheres: compulsive movements and automatic functions in which man has no acquisition or volition, like the movement of growth and what follows it of strength, old age, and weakness, like birth and death, and like various emotions of love, hatred, hunger, thirst, fear, and terror. As for the second type, they are actions arising from a wondrous secret specific to man which God Almighty deposited in him; we call it: choice and will. For the will of God Almighty has been attached to planting this secret in man's essence, which is the axis of moral responsibility in him, and to making him in many of his actions issue from this secret, by which he is called free and choosing. The meaning of that is that the will of God Almighty has been attached to you being a willing one, so the will of God Almighty -- thereby -- permeates to every action you will and choose. And thus it is impossible for any conflict to occur between the will of God Almighty and what you choose through your own will. For if we were to suppose that God is not willing for an action you have chosen by your will, then that means that He, glorified and exalted, is not willing for your will which directed you to that action, and this contradicts what has been established that God Almighty has willed for you to be willing and has willed to create in you this secret. So the supposition that God might not will the action you choose is proven false.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage explains the compatibility between human free will and divine will. Buti sets up the problem: how can human will exist alongside God's absolute will? He identifies two types of human actions: involuntary (like growth, emotions) and voluntary (based on choice). The voluntary actions spring from a 'wondrous secret' deposited by God: the capacity for choice and will. Buti argues that God's will has decreed that humans be willing agents, so God's will encompasses all human choices. Therefore, no conflict arises because if God did not will a particular human action, He would also not will the human will that leads to it, but He has willed that human will to exist. Thus, any apparent conflict is resolved by understanding that God's will includes the human will as a created capacity.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الاختيار
التكليف
السر العجيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human free will is independent of God's will and can conflict with it.
Correction: Buti shows that God's will includes the very capacity for choice, so every human choice is encompassed by divine will. There is no independence in the sense of being outside God's will; rather, God's will has decreed that humans choose freely.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, why is there no conflict between God's will and human free will?
A) Because human will is independent of God's will.
B) Because God's will includes the human capacity to choose, so God wills all that we choose.
C) Because God only wills some human actions.
D) Because human will is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
Human free will is not opposed to divine will because God has willed that humans choose freely, and thus all human choices fall under His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision—especially one that leads to sin—do you genuinely believe that God willed your ability to choose that action? How does that affect your sense of responsibility and your reliance on His mercy?
?
Is there an area of your life where you resist submitting to God's will, insisting that your own choice is independent? What would change if you fully embraced the truth that your freedom itself is a gift from Him?
?
If you truly internalized that every choice you make falls under God's will, would your fear of the future or your pride in past achievements dissolve? What concrete step can you take today to align your will more consciously with His?

The Difference Between Divine Will and Divine Approval
Buti distinguishes divine will from divine approval to resolve the problem of evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God can will sinful acts while still commanding against them and punishing them, because His will is distinct from His approval and command.

Core Idea
Divine will includes all events; divine approval only what is good.

This lesson tackles a common objection: if God wills everything, including sin, how can He punish us for it? Buti clarifies that the confusion arises from equating God's will with His command and pleasure. Using a verse from the Qur'an and a simple analogy, he shows that God's will encompasses all outcomes, but His approval and command are reserved for what is good. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine justice and predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Buti first presents the objection, then identifies the underlying misconception, and finally provides the answer with evidence.
The objection is about how God can punish what He wills.
2
Identify the key distinction
Focus on the difference between irāda (will) and riḍā (approval). Buti argues they are not the same. Find where he states this and how he proves it with the verse.
3
Analyze the servant analogy
Read the example of the master allowing his servant to act freely. Understand how the master wills the outcome even if he dislikes it. Compare this to God's will for sinful acts.
Buti then applies the same logic to a teacher-student example.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Ask yourself: How does this distinction solve the problem of evil? Why is there no contradiction between God's will and His command? Consider how this protects God's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين الإرادة والرضا : ولعلك تسأل بعد هذا : فكيف يعاقب الله الإنسان على فعل هو من مرادات الله عز وجل ؟! .. بل كيف يكون السلوك الذي نهى الله الإنسان عنه مراداً لله في الوقت نفسه ؟! والجواب أن هذا الإشكال فرع عن وهم ينبغي أن تحذر الوقوع فيه ، ألا وهو توهم أن الإرادة والأمر بمعنى واحد ، وأن الواحد منهما يستلزم الآخر . وهذا خطأ كبير في التقدير، فقد علمت فيما سبق أنه لا يقع شيء في الكون إلا بإرادته ، وإلا لكان ثمة ماهو موجود من فوق مشيئته واختياره ، وهو من أجلى مظاهر العجز والضعف التي ننزه الله عز وجل عنها ، وقد علمت أيضاً بأن الله يقول في كتابه و وَلا يَرضى لِعِبادِهِ الكُفْرَ ، وَإِنْ تَشْكُرُوا يَرْضَهُ لَكُمْ ) [ الزمر : ۷] وإذاً فكفر أبي جهل مثلا داخل في مرادات الله عز وجل ، كما ذكرناه ، ولكنه غير داخل فيما يُرضي الله عز وجل وفيما قد أمر به لدلالة الآية الصريحة على ذلك وفي مثال الخادم الذي ذكرته لك آنفاً ما ينبهك إلى هذه الحقيقة . فقد قلنا : إنك لم ترد إطلاق يده بالتصرف بمالك كما يشاء ، إلا وأنت مريد لظهور نتيجة ذلك أياً كانت النتيجة سواء كنت تحبها وترضاها أم لا .. وهذه حقيقة نلمسها جميعاً في تجاربنا وتصرفاتنا الشخصية ومعاملة بعضنا لبعض ، إنني بكل تأكيد ، لا أحب من تلميذي أن يكون مخفقاً في دراسته غير ناجح فيها ، وأظل أكرر على مسامعه الأمر بالدراسة وبذل الجهد ، ومع ذلك فأنا عندما أريد أن أختبره في نهاية العام، فإن إرادتي تسري من غير شك إلى النتيجة التي سيكشف عنها ذلك الاختيار ، وإذاً فأنا أريد بذلك ظهور النتيجة أياً كانت ، نجاحاً أم رسوباً ولا يمكن أن يعتقد عاقل من الناس أن تناقضاً قد وقع بين ماكنت آمره به من الاجتهاد وما أريده اليوم من النتيجة التي تفصح عن واقع أمره .
The difference between will (irāda) and pleasure (riḍā): Perhaps you ask after this: How does God punish a person for an act that is among the wills (murādāt) of God Almighty? … Rather, how can the behavior that God forbade the human be willed (murād) by God at the same time?! The answer is that this problem stems from a misconception that you should beware of falling into, namely the illusion that will (irāda) and command (amr) are the same, and that each entails the other. This is a great error in estimation. You have already learned from what preceded that nothing occurs in the universe except by His will, otherwise there would be something existing beyond His volition (mashīʾa) and choice (ikhtiyār), which is among the clearest manifestations of incapacity and weakness that we absolve God Almighty of. And you have also learned that God says in His Book: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief, and if you are grateful, He approves it for you' (az-Zumar 39:7). Therefore, the disbelief of Abu Jahl, for example, is included in the wills (murādāt) of God Almighty, as we mentioned, but it is not included in what pleases (yurḍī) God Almighty nor in what He has commanded, due to the clear indication of the verse. And in the example of the servant that I mentioned to you earlier, there is something that alerts you to this truth. For we said: You did not intend to let him act freely with your money as he wishes, except that you are willing (murīd) for the appearance of that result, whatever the result, whether you love and are pleased with it or not. And this is a truth that we all touch in our experiences, personal actions, and dealings with one another. I, with full certainty, do not love for my student to be failing in his studies, unsuccessful in them, and I keep repeating in his ears the command to study and exert effort. Yet, when I want to test him at the end of the year, my will undoubtedly extends to the result that this test will reveal. Therefore, I thereby will the appearance of the result, whatever it may be, success or failure. No rational person can think that a contradiction has occurred between what I commanded him of diligence and what I today will of the result that discloses his true state.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (shubha wa radd) format. The author addresses a potential objection: If God wills all events, including sinful acts, how can He punish humans for those acts? And how can an act be both forbidden by God and willed by Him? The answer begins by identifying the underlying misconception: confusing divine will (irāda) with divine command (amr) and pleasure (riḍā). These are distinct. The author repeats the earlier point that nothing occurs without God's will, otherwise something would exist outside His volition, implying imperfection. Then he cites Qur'an 39:7, which states that God does not approve (lā yarḍā) of disbelief, but if people are grateful, He approves it. From this, the distinction is clear: the disbelief of Abu Jahl is under God's will (irāda) but not under His approval (riḍā) or command. The author returns to the servant analogy to illustrate: the master wills the outcome of the test, whether good or bad, but he only commands and loves the good outcome. This is analogous to a teacher who commands his student to study and dislikes failure, but when he tests the student, his will extends to whatever result the test reveals. There is no contradiction between commanding effort and willing the outcome that discloses the student's actual condition. Thus, God's will for sinful acts does not mean He commands or approves them; rather, His will is that they occur as part of His overall plan, while His command and pleasure are only for good.

Key Terms
إرادة
رضا
أمر
مراد
يرضى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will and His command are identical; if God wills an act, He must approve and command it.
Correction: God's will (irāda) encompasses all events, including evil, but His command (amr) and approval (riḍā) are only for good acts. The Qur'an and rational analogy confirm this distinction.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, why is the disbelief of Abu Jahl willed by God but not approved by Him?
A) Because God is not powerful enough to prevent disbelief.
B) Because God's will and approval are identical, so disbelief must be approved.
C) Because God's will includes all events, but His approval only applies to good acts and obedience.
D) Because God's will is limited to what He loves.

Key Takeaway
God's will covers all events, but His approval and command are only for what is good; this resolves the apparent contradiction of God willing sin while punishing it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful trial or witness evil in the world, do you instinctively feel that God has abandoned you or that He is unjust? How does Buti’s distinction between God’s will and His approval change your perspective on such hardship? Will you now strive to seek His approval even when His will brings difficulty?
?
Reflect on a time you committed a sin you knew was forbidden. Did you ever quietly think, 'If God didn’t will it, it wouldn’t have happened, so maybe it’s not so bad'? How does understanding that God wills what He hates force you to take personal responsibility for your actions? Will you repent with sincerity, knowing God does not approve of your sin?
?
Buti uses the example of a teacher who wills the test result but only commands diligence. Where in your life have you made excuses for disobedience by attributing it to God’s will? Will you commit today to aligning your actions with God’s command and approval, regardless of the outcomes He wills?

The Distinction Between Will and Command/Pleasure
Buti introduces the objection and presents the core distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine will (irāda) is distinct from divine command (amr) and divine pleasure (riḍā), and that God may will something without commanding it or being pleased with it.

Core Idea
Will is not the same as command or pleasure.

Buti begins by addressing a natural objection: how can God punish humans for actions that He Himself wills? He immediately identifies the root cause—confusing divine will with divine command or pleasure. This lesson focuses on that key distinction and its scriptural basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where Buti poses the question and gives the initial answer. Notice how he identifies the misconception.
The objection is a common one: if God wills everything, then why punish?
2
Identify the three terms
Identify the three Arabic terms: إرادة (will), أمر (command), رضا (pleasure). Understand that Buti argues these are not equivalent.
3
Reflect on the Qur'anic evidence
Buti cites verse 39:7 to show that God is not pleased with disbelief, even though it occurs by His will. Ponder how this verse supports the distinction.
The verse explicitly states that God does not approve of disbelief.

الفرق بين الإرادة والرضا :
ولعلك تسأل بعد هذا : فكيف يعاقب الله الإنسان على فعل هو من مرادات الله عز وجل ؟! .. بل كيف يكون السلوك الذي نهى الله الإنسان عنه مراداً لله في الوقت نفسه ؟!
والجواب أن هذا الإشكال فرع عن وهم ينبغي أن تحذر الوقوع فيه ، ألا وهو توهم أن الإرادة والأمر بمعنى واحد ، وأن الواحد منهما يستلزم الآخر . وهذا خطأ كبير في التقدير، فقد علمت فيما سبق أنه لا يقع شيء في الكون إلا بإرادته ، وإلا لكان ثمة ماهو موجود من فوق مشيئته واختياره ، وهو من أجلى مظاهر العجز والضعف التي ننزه الله عز وجل عنها ، وقد علمت أيضاً بأن الله يقول في كتابه ﴿ وَلا يَرضى لِعِبادِهِ الكُفْرَ ، وَإِنْ تَشْكُرُوا يَرْضَهُ لَكُمْ ﴾ [ الزمر : ۷] وإذاً فكفر أبي جهل مثلا داخل في مرادات الله عز وجل ، كما ذكرناه ، ولكنه غير داخل فيما يُرضي الله عز وجل وفيما قد أمر به لدلالة الآية الصريحة على ذلك
وفي مثال الخادم الذي ذكرته لك آنفاً ما ينبهك إلى هذه الحقيقة . فقد قلنا : إنك لم ترد إطلاق يده بالتصرف بمالك كما يشاء ، إلا وأنت مريد لظهور نتيجة ذلك أياً كانت النتيجة سواء كنت تحبها وترضاها أم لا .. وهذه حقيقة نلمسها جميعاً في تجاربنا وتصرفاتنا الشخصية ومعاملة بعضنا لبعض ، إنني بكل تأكيد ، لا أحب من تلميذي أن يكون مخفقاً في دراسته غير ناجح فيها ، وأظل أكرر على مسامعه الأمر بالدراسة وبذل الجهد ، ومع ذلك فأنا عندما أريد أن أختبره في نهاية العام، فإن إرادتي تسري من غير شك إلى النتيجة التي سيكشف عنها ذلك الاختيار ، وإذاً فأنا أريد بذلك ظهور النتيجة أياً كانت ، نجاحاً أم رسوباً ولا يمكن أن يعتقد عاقل من الناس أن تناقضاً قد وقع بين ماكنت آمره به من الاجتهاد وما أريده اليوم من النتيجة التي تفصح عن واقع أمره .
وهكذا ينبغي أن تعلم ، بأن الإرادة لاتستلزم الأمر ولا الرضا بالشيء المراد . وهذه أيضاً من الزلات التي تاه فيها المعتزلة واضطربت أقوالهم فيها بين كرّ وفر .
فإذا أمعنت في هذا الذي ذكرناه ، أدركت أن الإنسان ، في كل أعماله وتصرفاته الاختيارية إنما يتحرك في دائرة الإرادة الإلهية لا يتخطاها وأدركت أيضاً أنه لا تنافي بين كون الإنسان مختاراً مريداً في تصرفاتـه هـذه وبين كونـه لا يتخطى الإرادة الإلهية ، وليس الأمر كما يظن بعض السطحيين أن فعل الإنسان مادام حاصلا بإرادة الله ، فليس له فيه إذا حرية ولا كسب ، ليس الأمر كذلك إلا إذا صح أن يقول التلميذ الراسب في امتحانه للأستاذ الذي امتحنه : : إنني مقهور على هذا الرسوب ، لأنك قد أردت مني الرسوب عندما أردت امتحاني ، وإلا إذا صح أن يقول الخادم لمخدومه : إنني مقهور على مابدر مني من الخيانة في معاملتك ، تحت سلطان إرادتك التي توجهت إلى اختباري وإطلاق يدي في التصرف بمالك . وبدهي أن أحداً من العقلاء لا يقول هذا الكلام ولا يقبل أن يسمعه
وفي بيان هذه الحقيقة يقول العلامة سعد الدين التفتنازاني على العقائد : ) فإن قيل : بعد تعميم على الله تعالى وإرادته ، الجبر لازم قطعاً ، لأنها إما أن يتعلقا بوجوب الفعل فيجب أو بعدمه فيمتنع ، ولا اختيار مع الـوجـوب والامتناع - قلنا : يعلم ويريد أن العبد يفعله أو يتركــه بــاختياره ، فلا إشكال (1) . فإن قلت ، فهذا الذي تقوله مقنع ومفهوم ، لولا أن في القرآن آية تبطل هذا المعنى الذي تقول ، إذ تدل على أن الإنسان لا يملك لنفسه أي مشيئة إلا بإذن الله ومشيئته ، وهي قوله تعالى ﴿ وَما تَشاؤون إلا أَنْ يَشَاءَ اللهُ إِنَّ اللهَ كانَ عَليماً ﴾
(۱) شرح العقائد النسفية ص ٣٥٤ .
The difference between will and pleasure: Perhaps you will ask after this: How does God punish a human for an act that is among God's wills (murādāt Allāh)?! ... Rather, how can behavior that God forbade to the human be willed (murādan) by God at the same time?! The answer is that this problem stems from a misconception that you should beware of falling into, namely imagining that will (al-irāda) and command (al-amr) are one and the same, and that each one entails the other. This is a great error in estimation. For you have learned previously that nothing occurs in the universe except by His will (irādatihī); otherwise there would be something existing beyond His will and choice, and that is among the clearest manifestations of incapacity and weakness that we declare God Most High to be above. And you have also learned that God says in His Book: 'And He is not pleased (yarḍā) for His servants disbelief, and if you are grateful He is pleased (yarḍahu) for you' [Az-Zumar: 7]. Thus, the disbelief of Abī Jahl, for example, is included among God's wills (murādāt Allāh) as we mentioned, but it is not included in what pleases (yurḍī) God Most High nor in what He commanded, due to the clear indication of the verse. And in the example of the servant that I mentioned to you earlier, there is something to alert you to this truth. We said: You did not intend to let him loose to dispose of your wealth as he wishes, except that you willed (murīd) the appearance of the result whatever the result, whether you love it and are pleased with it or not. This is a truth that we all touch in our experiences and personal actions and our interactions with one another. I certainly do not love that my student be failing in his studies, not successful in it, and I keep repeating to him the command to study and exert effort. Nevertheless, when I want to test him at the end of the year, my will (irādatī) undoubtedly extends to the result that that test will reveal. And so, I will thereby the appearance of the result whatever it is, success or failure. No reasonable person can think that a contradiction has occurred between what I was commanding him of diligence and what I want today of the result that discloses the reality of his situation. Thus you should know that will (al-irāda) does not entail command (al-amr) nor pleasure (ar-riḍā) with the willed thing. This is also among the slips in which the Mu'tazila became lost and their statements became confused therein, going back and forth. So if you ponder what we have mentioned, you will realize that the human, in all his voluntary acts and actions, moves within the circle of divine will (al-irāda al-ilāhiyya) and does not overstep it. And you will also realize that there is no contradiction between the human being being free, willing in these actions and his not overstepping divine will. It is not as some superficial people imagine: that as long as the human act occurs by God's will, then the human has no freedom (ḥurriyya) nor acquisition (kasb) in it. It is not like that, unless it would be valid for the failing student in his exam to say to the teacher who examined him: 'I was compelled (maqhūr) to this failure, because you willed failure for me when you willed to test me.' Or unless it would be valid for the servant to say to his master: 'I was compelled to what emanated from me of betrayal in your dealing, under the authority of your will that was directed to testing me and giving me free rein to dispose of your wealth.' It is obvious that no rational person would say this statement nor accept to hear it. In clarifying this truth, the scholar Sa'd al-Dīn al-Taftazānī says in his commentary on al-'Aqā'id: 'For if it is said: After affirming generality of God's will (irādatihi), compulsion (al-jabr) is necessarily entailed, because it (i.e., the will) either attaches to the obligation of the act, so it is necessary, or to its non-existence, so it is impossible, and there is no choice (ikhtiyār) with necessity and impossibility — we say: He knows and wills that the servant does or leaves it by his choice (bi-ikhtiyārihī), so there is no problem...' If you say: 'This that you say is convincing and understandable, were it not for a verse in the Qur'an that nullifies this meaning that you say, for it indicates that the human possesses no will of his own except by God's permission and will, and that is His saying: 'And you do not will except that God wills; indeed God is all-knowing' — then the answer is already given in the text. (1) Sharḥ al-'Aqā'id al-Nasafiyya, p. 354.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer clarification by Buti on the relationship between divine will (irāda) and human responsibility. It begins with the objection: 'How can God punish humans for actions that He Himself willed?' The objection is based on a misunderstanding — namely, equating divine will with divine command (amr) or divine pleasure (riḍā). Buti explains that these are distinct: God wills everything that happens in the universe, including disbelief, but He does not command it nor is He pleased with it. He cites the Qur'anic verse (39:7) to show that God is not pleased with disbelief. Then he gives two analogies: a master giving a servant free rein but not necessarily liking the outcome, and a teacher testing a student — the teacher wills the test result whatever it is, but earlier had commanded the student to study. These show that will does not entail command or pleasure. Buti then warns that this confusion led the Mu'tazila astray. He concludes that humans act voluntarily within the sphere of divine will, and this does not negate their freedom or acquisition (kasb). To further support, he quotes al-Taftazānī from Sharḥ al-'Aqā'id al-Nasafiyya: Taftazānī addresses the objection that making divine will universal leads to compulsion (jabr). The reply is that God knows and wills that the servant does or leaves an act by his own choice — thus no contradiction. This refutes the claim that human will is negated by divine will, even though the Qur'an states that humans will nothing except as God wills.

Key Terms
إرادة
رضا
أمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine will and divine command/pleasure are the same, so if God wills something He must also command and approve of it.
Correction: Will (irāda) is a broader category than command (amr) and pleasure (riḍā). God wills all events, but only commands and is pleased with good deeds.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction Buti makes to resolve the apparent contradiction between God willing all things and punishing humans for their actions?
A) God's will only extends to good actions, not evil ones.
B) God's will is identical to His command, but He commands both good and evil.
C) God's will does not entail His command or pleasure; He may will something without commanding it or being pleased with it.
D) Humans have absolute free will independent of God's will.

Key Takeaway
God's will for an event does not mean He commands or approves of it; this distinction preserves divine sovereignty and human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you did something you knew was wrong, yet you later made excuses, thinking, 'If God didn't want it to happen, it wouldn't have happened.' How does this lesson dismantle that excuse and place full responsibility back on you?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you secretly blame God for willing it? What does the distinction between will and pleasure demand of your heart when you face such evil today?
?
If God is not pleased with your sin, yet He willed it, what does that say about His patience and your opportunity to repent? Will you let this realization crush your arrogance or harden your heart further?

Analogies Illustrating the Distinction
Buti provides two analogies to illustrate the distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between will and pleasure using the analogies of the master-servant and teacher-student.

Core Idea
Analogies show that will does not equal approval.

Buti reinforces the distinction with two everyday analogies: a master giving a servant free rein over his wealth, and a teacher testing a student. In both cases, the person wills the outcome (whatever it may be) without necessarily loving or approving of it. These analogies make the abstract theological point concrete and relatable.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogies
Read the Arabic passage containing the two analogies. Note how Buti sets up each scenario and draws the parallel to divine will.
The analogies are: (1) master-servant, (2) teacher-student.
2
Analyze the master-servant example
The master wills the servant to act freely, but that does not mean he is pleased with any resulting mismanagement. Identify the core lesson.
3
Analyze the teacher-student example
The teacher commands the student to study, but when testing, the teacher wills the outcome (success or failure) without approving of failure. See the parallel.
The teacher's will to test does not cancel the command to study.

الفرق بين الإرادة والرضا :
ولعلك تسأل بعد هذا : فكيف يعاقب الله الإنسان على فعل هو من مرادات الله عز وجل ؟! .. بل كيف يكون السلوك الذي نهى الله الإنسان عنه مراداً لله في الوقت نفسه ؟!
والجواب أن هذا الإشكال فرع عن وهم ينبغي أن تحذر الوقوع فيه ، ألا وهو توهم أن الإرادة والأمر بمعنى واحد ، وأن الواحد منهما يستلزم الآخر . وهذا خطأ كبير في التقدير، فقد علمت فيما سبق أنه لا يقع شيء في الكون إلا بإرادته ، وإلا لكان ثمة ماهو موجود من فوق مشيئته واختياره ، وهو من أجلى مظاهر العجز والضعف التي ننزه الله عز وجل عنها ، وقد علمت أيضاً بأن الله يقول في كتابه ﴿ وَلا يَرضى لِعِبادِهِ الكُفْرَ ، وَإِنْ تَشْكُرُوا يَرْضَهُ لَكُمْ ﴾ [ الزمر : ۷] وإذاً فكفر أبي جهل مثلا داخل في مرادات الله عز وجل ، كما ذكرناه ، ولكنه غير داخل فيما يُرضي الله عز وجل وفيما قد أمر به لدلالة الآية الصريحة على ذلك
وفي مثال الخادم الذي ذكرته لك آنفاً ما ينبهك إلى هذه الحقيقة . فقد قلنا : إنك لم ترد إطلاق يده بالتصرف بمالك كما يشاء ، إلا وأنت مريد لظهور نتيجة ذلك أياً كانت النتيجة سواء كنت تحبها وترضاها أم لا .. وهذه حقيقة نلمسها جميعاً في تجاربنا وتصرفاتنا الشخصية ومعاملة بعضنا لبعض ، إنني بكل تأكيد ، لا أحب من تلميذي أن يكون مخفقاً في دراسته غير ناجح فيها ، وأظل أكرر على مسامعه الأمر بالدراسة وبذل الجهد ، ومع ذلك فأنا عندما أريد أن أختبره في نهاية العام، فإن إرادتي تسري من غير شك إلى النتيجة التي سيكشف عنها ذلك الاختيار ، وإذاً فأنا أريد بذلك ظهور النتيجة أياً كانت ، نجاحاً أم رسوباً ولا يمكن أن يعتقد عاقل من الناس أن تناقضاً قد وقع بين ماكنت آمره به من الاجتهاد وما أريده اليوم من النتيجة التي تفصح عن واقع أمره .
وهكذا ينبغي أن تعلم ، بأن الإرادة لاتستلزم الأمر ولا الرضا بالشيء المراد . وهذه أيضاً من الزلات التي تاه فيها المعتزلة واضطربت أقوالهم فيها بين كرّ وفر .
فإذا أمعنت في هذا الذي ذكرناه ، أدركت أن الإنسان ، في كل أعماله وتصرفاته الاختيارية إنما يتحرك في دائرة الإرادة الإلهية لا يتخطاها وأدركت أيضاً أنه لا تنافي بين كون الإنسان مختاراً مريداً في تصرفاتـه هـذه وبين كونـه لا يتخطى الإرادة الإلهية ، وليس الأمر كما يظن بعض السطحيين أن فعل الإنسان مادام حاصلا بإرادة الله ، فليس له فيه إذا حرية ولا كسب ، ليس الأمر كذلك إلا إذا صح أن يقول التلميذ الراسب في امتحانه للأستاذ الذي امتحنه : : إنني مقهور على هذا الرسوب ، لأنك قد أردت مني الرسوب عندما أردت امتحاني ، وإلا إذا صح أن يقول الخادم لمخدومه : إنني مقهور على مابدر مني من الخيانة في معاملتك ، تحت سلطان إرادتك التي توجهت إلى اختباري وإطلاق يدي في التصرف بمالك . وبدهي أن أحداً من العقلاء لا يقول هذا الكلام ولا يقبل أن يسمعه
وفي بيان هذه الحقيقة يقول العلامة سعد الدين التفتنازاني على العقائد : ) فإن قيل : بعد تعميم على الله تعالى وإرادته ، الجبر لازم قطعاً ، لأنها إما أن يتعلقا بوجوب الفعل فيجب أو بعدمه فيمتنع ، ولا اختيار مع الـوجـوب والامتناع - قلنا : يعلم ويريد أن العبد يفعله أو يتركــه بــاختياره ، فلا إشكال (1) . فإن قلت ، فهذا الذي تقوله مقنع ومفهوم ، لولا أن في القرآن آية تبطل هذا المعنى الذي تقول ، إذ تدل على أن الإنسان لا يملك لنفسه أي مشيئة إلا بإذن الله ومشيئته ، وهي قوله تعالى ﴿ وَما تَشاؤون إلا أَنْ يَشَاءَ اللهُ إِنَّ اللهَ كانَ عَليماً ﴾
(۱) شرح العقائد النسفية ص ٣٥٤ .
The difference between will and pleasure: Perhaps you will ask after this: How does God punish a human for an act that is among God's wills (murādāt Allāh)?! ... Rather, how can behavior that God forbade to the human be willed (murādan) by God at the same time?! The answer is that this problem stems from a misconception that you should beware of falling into, namely imagining that will (al-irāda) and command (al-amr) are one and the same, and that each one entails the other. This is a great error in estimation. For you have learned previously that nothing occurs in the universe except by His will (irādatihī); otherwise there would be something existing beyond His will and choice, and that is among the clearest manifestations of incapacity and weakness that we declare God Most High to be above. And you have also learned that God says in His Book: 'And He is not pleased (yarḍā) for His servants disbelief, and if you are grateful He is pleased (yarḍahu) for you' [Az-Zumar: 7]. Thus, the disbelief of Abī Jahl, for example, is included among God's wills (murādāt Allāh) as we mentioned, but it is not included in what pleases (yurḍī) God Most High nor in what He commanded, due to the clear indication of the verse. And in the example of the servant that I mentioned to you earlier, there is something to alert you to this truth. We said: You did not intend to let him loose to dispose of your wealth as he wishes, except that you willed (murīd) the appearance of the result whatever the result, whether you love it and are pleased with it or not. This is a truth that we all touch in our experiences and personal actions and our interactions with one another. I certainly do not love that my student be failing in his studies, not successful in it, and I keep repeating to him the command to study and exert effort. Nevertheless, when I want to test him at the end of the year, my will (irādatī) undoubtedly extends to the result that that test will reveal. And so, I will thereby the appearance of the result whatever it is, success or failure. No reasonable person can think that a contradiction has occurred between what I was commanding him of diligence and what I want today of the result that discloses the reality of his situation. Thus you should know that will (al-irāda) does not entail command (al-amr) nor pleasure (ar-riḍā) with the willed thing. This is also among the slips in which the Mu'tazila became lost and their statements became confused therein, going back and forth. So if you ponder what we have mentioned, you will realize that the human, in all his voluntary acts and actions, moves within the circle of divine will (al-irāda al-ilāhiyya) and does not overstep it. And you will also realize that there is no contradiction between the human being being free, willing in these actions and his not overstepping divine will. It is not as some superficial people imagine: that as long as the human act occurs by God's will, then the human has no freedom (ḥurriyya) nor acquisition (kasb) in it. It is not like that, unless it would be valid for the failing student in his exam to say to the teacher who examined him: 'I was compelled (maqhūr) to this failure, because you willed failure for me when you willed to test me.' Or unless it would be valid for the servant to say to his master: 'I was compelled to what emanated from me of betrayal in your dealing, under the authority of your will that was directed to testing me and giving me free rein to dispose of your wealth.' It is obvious that no rational person would say this statement nor accept to hear it. In clarifying this truth, the scholar Sa'd al-Dīn al-Taftazānī says in his commentary on al-'Aqā'id: 'For if it is said: After affirming generality of God's will (irādatihi), compulsion (al-jabr) is necessarily entailed, because it (i.e., the will) either attaches to the obligation of the act, so it is necessary, or to its non-existence, so it is impossible, and there is no choice (ikhtiyār) with necessity and impossibility — we say: He knows and wills that the servant does or leaves it by his choice (bi-ikhtiyārihī), so there is no problem...' If you say: 'This that you say is convincing and understandable, were it not for a verse in the Qur'an that nullifies this meaning that you say, for it indicates that the human possesses no will of his own except by God's permission and will, and that is His saying: 'And you do not will except that God wills; indeed God is all-knowing' — then the answer is already given in the text. (1) Sharḥ al-'Aqā'id al-Nasafiyya, p. 354.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer clarification by Buti on the relationship between divine will (irāda) and human responsibility. It begins with the objection: 'How can God punish humans for actions that He Himself willed?' The objection is based on a misunderstanding — namely, equating divine will with divine command (amr) or divine pleasure (riḍā). Buti explains that these are distinct: God wills everything that happens in the universe, including disbelief, but He does not command it nor is He pleased with it. He cites the Qur'anic verse (39:7) to show that God is not pleased with disbelief. Then he gives two analogies: a master giving a servant free rein but not necessarily liking the outcome, and a teacher testing a student — the teacher wills the test result whatever it is, but earlier had commanded the student to study. These show that will does not entail command or pleasure. Buti then warns that this confusion led the Mu'tazila astray. He concludes that humans act voluntarily within the sphere of divine will, and this does not negate their freedom or acquisition (kasb). To further support, he quotes al-Taftazānī from Sharḥ al-'Aqā'id al-Nasafiyya: Taftazānī addresses the objection that making divine will universal leads to compulsion (jabr). The reply is that God knows and wills that the servant does or leaves an act by his own choice — thus no contradiction. This refutes the claim that human will is negated by divine will, even though the Qur'an states that humans will nothing except as God wills.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Human Freedom Within Divine Will
Buti concludes with the compatibility of human freedom and divine will, citing Taftazani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that human free will and divine will are compatible, and that the objection of compulsion (jabr) is refuted by the fact that God wills the servant's own choice.

Core Idea
God wills that humans act by their own choice.

Buti concludes by addressing the concern that if all actions are willed by God, then humans are compelled (jabr). He uses the student–teacher example again to show that compulsion is not entailed, and then quotes Taftazani to provide a scholarly resolution: God knows and wills that the servant does or leaves an act by his own choice. This lesson ties together the entire discussion on divine will and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding section
Read the Arabic from 'فإذا أمعنت' to the end. Notice how Buti applies the earlier distinction to the question of human freedom.
Focus on the phrase: 'يعلم ويريد أن العبد يفعله أو يتركه باختياره'.
2
Analyze the compulsion objection
Understand the objection: if God's will is universal, then whatever happens is necessary (jabr), leaving no room for choice. Buti and Taftazani reject this by saying that God's will includes the fact that the human chooses.
The key is that God's will attaches to the act with the attribute of human choice.
3
Appreciate the resolution
The resolution is that God's will does not negate human choice because God's will encompasses the reality of the human's own free choice.
